\begin{itemize}

\[

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Briefly note any fragile steps, missing cases, or unsupported claims; if the proof is correct, say why the key steps hold.
</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

So he can try to increase S_even, but the increase in Q may limit how much he can increase. So we need to analyze the game in terms of "bounding" the sum.

Let's formalize the game state. We have sequences (x_n). At each step, the player chooses x_n. The game ends when a player cannot move. So we need to see who can force the other to be unable to move.

Define the "potential" functions. Since the constraints are cumulative, the game can be seen as a kind of "race" where each player tries to push the other over the bound.

We can attempt to find necessary and sufficient conditions for infinite play, and then see which player can force a win.

Observation: The inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n always holds (by Cauchy). So Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. Also, S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n). So if a player wants to cause the other to lose, they can try to make the appropriate cumulative sum exceed the bound.

We can think of the game as a two-player game with state (S_n, Q_n) after each turn, but the constraints are only on odd n for S and even n for Q. Actually the constraints are: at odd n, S_n ≤ λ n; at even n, Q_n ≤ n. So the state after each turn must satisfy the constraint for that turn. But the constraints are only checked at the end of the turn? The rules: On nth turn, the player chooses x_n such that the cumulative sum (or sum of squares) is ≤ λ n (or n). So after the choice, the inequality must hold. So the state after turn n must satisfy the constraint for that n. So the game proceeds if after each turn the inequality holds.

So we can define the state after each turn as (S_n, Q_n). For odd n, we require S_n ≤ λ n. For even n, we require Q_n ≤ n. Also, the numbers are nonnegative.

The players are adversarial. The outcome can be determined by analyzing the "boundary" in the (S,Q) plane. This is reminiscent of a game where players alternately push a point (S,Q) in the plane, with constraints that S is nondecreasing, Q is nondecreasing, and the point must stay within certain regions on each turn. However, note that the constraints are only applied at the turn when the index is odd or even. So the state after an odd turn must be in region R_odd = { (S,Q) : S ≤ λ n, Q ≥ 0, S ≥ 0 } and after an even turn, R_even = { (S,Q) : Q ≤ n, S ≥ 0 }.

But also the move changes both S and Q. So we can treat it as a game where players choose increments (dx, dq) = (x_n, x_n^2) subject to the turn constraint. The state evolves.

Alice's moves: on odd n, she chooses x ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1}+x ≤ λ n. So the new S = S_{n-1}+x ≤ λ n. She can choose any x up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So her choice is a nonnegative increment that brings S up to some value ≤ λ n. She also increases Q by x^2. So she can control how much Q increases.

Bazza's moves: on even n, he chooses x ≥ 0 such that Q_{n-1}+x^2 ≤ n. So his new Q = Q_{n-1}+x^2 ≤ n. He can choose any x up to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So his increment in S is x, and in Q is x^2.

Thus, the game is a kind of "alternating" game where each player can increase S and Q, but with different bounds.

We need to find λ such that Alice can force a win, and λ such that Bazza can force a win.

Let's analyze the recurrence for Q_odd when Alice takes max.

Let’s denote the sequence of indices: n=1,3,5,... odd. For each odd n=2k+1, Alice sets x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. Then S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Then Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} such that Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2. He can choose any x in [0, sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1})]. Let’s denote a_k = Q_{2k+1} (sum of squares after Alice's move at odd 2k+1). Also, let b_k = S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Then after Bazza's move, Q_{2k+2} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2, and S_{2k+2} = b_k + x_{2k+2}. Then on the next odd turn, Alice will choose x_{2k+3} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+3) - (b_k + x_{2k+2}). Then S_{2k+3}=λ(2k+3). And a_{k+1} = Q_{2k+3} = Q_{2k+2} + x_{2k+3}^2 = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (λ(2k+3) - b_k - x_{2k+2})^2.

Since b_k = λ(2k+1). So we have a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (λ(2k+3) - λ(2k+1) - x_{2k+2})^2 = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2.

Thus, the recurrence for a_k (the sum of squares after Alice's move at odd index 2k+1) is:

a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2,

where x_{2k+2} is Bazza's chosen x on even turn 2k+2, and must satisfy x_{2k+2} ≥ 0 and also Q_{2k+2} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2 (since Bazza's move must satisfy Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2). So the constraint is a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2. Also, note that x_{2k+2} is chosen by Bazza, and he wants to eventually cause Alice to lose. The condition for Alice to lose on her next turn (n=2k+3) is S_{2k+2} > λ(2k+3). Since S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + x_{2k+2}. So condition is λ(2k+1)+x_{2k+2} > λ(2k+3) => x_{2k+2} > 2λ. So Bazza wants to choose x_{2k+2} > 2λ if possible, while respecting a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2. If he can do that, he wins immediately. If not, he might try to force a win later by making a_k large enough that later he can exceed 2λ? But if he cannot exceed 2λ now, he might choose a smaller x, and then later the condition might become easier? Because as k increases, the bound on x_{2k+2} is sqrt(2k+2 - a_k). So if a_k is small, he can choose large x. But Alice's strategy may increase a_k quickly, making the bound small. So we need to analyze the dynamics.

We can view the game as a kind of "optimal play" where both players choose moves to maximize their chances. But we need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., can force a win regardless of Bazza's choices). This is like a "positional" game: Alice can guarantee a win if she can make a move that leads to a position where no matter what Bazza does, she can eventually win. She can also use strategies other than max.

Given that for λ > 1, Alice can win by taking max on first move? Actually for λ>sqrt(2), she wins immediately. For λ between 1 and sqrt(2), we saw λ=1.2 she wins immediately on first move? We need to find the outcome of the game for each λ. This is a typical "boundary" game where players alternately push a point (S,Q) with constraints. The game is determined by the inequality that must hold at each turn. We can analyze using "potential" or "strategy" to see who can force a win.

Let's denote the state after each turn. Since the constraints are only on odd n for S and even n for Q, we can think of the game as a "race" between the two constraints. For a given λ, we can ask: Is there a strategy for Alice to ensure that Bazza cannot move eventually? She can try to make Q_{odd} > next even index, i.e., Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. Similarly, Bazza can try to make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1). So the game reduces to whether one player can force the other's cumulative sum to exceed a certain bound.

We can analyze the game as a "positional" game where each player chooses the increment to maximize their chances. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" game where the two players have different "budgets". Possibly the critical λ is 1? But we saw λ=0.8, Alice can survive with max, but can she force a win? Perhaps she can force a win by playing a different strategy, not necessarily max. Actually to make Q_odd > even index, she needs to choose x_n such that after her move, Q_n > next even index. Since Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, and she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. She could try to make x_n as large as possible, but that might be limited by S. If S_{n-1} is small, she can take large x. However, Bazza can also influence S_{n-1} by his choices. So it's a game of balancing.

- If 1.5λ is in [0, sqrt(2)], then min = 4.5λ^2.
- If 1.5λ > sqrt(2), then the minimum occurs at x2=sqrt(2) because the function is decreasing on [0, sqrt(2)]? Actually f'(x)=4x-6λ, so if λ > 2sqrt(2)/3? Not needed; we can consider cases.

We need f(x2) > 4 for all allowed x2. So the condition is that the minimum over the interval is >4.

Define the game after each of Alice's moves (odd n). Let's denote state after her move at odd n=2k+1. She sets S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Let a_k = Q_{2k+1} (sum of squares after her move). Then Bazza's move at even n=2k+2: he chooses x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - a_k)], and then S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + x_{2k+2}, Q_{2k+2} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2. Then Alice's next move at n=2k+3: she chooses x_{2k+3} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2}, which is the maximum allowed (since she wants to maximize Q). So x_{2k+3} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + x_{2k+2}) = 2λ - x_{2k+2}. Then Q_{2k+3} = Q_{2k+2} + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2 = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2.

Thus, the recurrence for a_k is:

a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2,

where x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - a_k)] and also must satisfy that after Alice's move, the sum constraint holds (which we already used). This recurrence defines the evolution of Q after Alice's moves. For the game to continue indefinitely, we need that for each k, there is a choice x_{2k+2} such that a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2. Since a_k is increasing, we need that the bound sqrt(2k+2 - a_k) is nonnegative. If at some k, we have a_k > 2k+2, then Bazza loses immediately because Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so he cannot move. So Alice wants to make a_k > 2k+2 at some k. If she can guarantee that, she wins. Conversely, Bazza wants to keep a_k ≤ 2k+2 so that he can move. If he can always choose x_{2k+2} to satisfy the inequality, the game may continue.

Thus, the outcome depends on whether Alice can force a_k to exceed 2k+2 at some k, regardless of Bazza's choices. Since Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} to minimize the chance of that, Alice chooses her moves (x_{2k+1}) to maximize the chance. In the strategy we considered, she always takes x_{2k+1} = 2λ - x_{2k} (i.e., the maximum possible). This gives a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2. She can also choose a smaller x_{2k+1}? But a_k = Q_{2k+1} would be smaller if she chooses a smaller x_{2k+1} (since x_{2k+1}^2 would be smaller). So that would be worse for her because Q is smaller. So she should always take the maximum x allowed to maximize Q. So the max strategy is optimal for her.

Thus, the game reduces to: Starting with a_0 = Q_1 = x_1^2, where x_1 is chosen by Alice on her first turn. She can choose any x_1 ∈ [0, λ]. Then for k=0,1,2,..., Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - a_k)], and then a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2. The game continues as long as a_k ≤ 2k+2 for each k. If at some k, a_k > 2k+2, then Bazza loses on his next turn (n=2k+2) because Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so he cannot move. So Alice wins if she can force a_k > 2k+2 for some k.

From the above, for λ=0.85, with x1=0.85, a1 = 0.7225 + (1.130^2 + (2.55-1.130)^2) = 0.7225+ (1.2775+1.42^2=1.2775+2.0164=3.2939) = 4.0164 > 4, win. For λ=0.82, a1 = 0.6724 + (1.3276+1.294^2=1.3276+1.674=3.0016) = 3.674 < 4, no win. So the threshold is where a1 = 4 exactly when x1=λ.

We need to solve for λ such that: a0 = λ^2, and Bazza chooses x2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2) (since that gives the minimum a1? Actually we need to minimize a1 = λ^2 + x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2, subject to x2 ∈ [0, sqrt(2-λ^2)]. The minimal a1 is achieved at the maximum possible x2 if that is less than the unconstrained minimum, because the function is decreasing for x<1.5λ. So the minimal a1 is at x2 = sqrt(2-λ^2) (the maximum allowed), giving a1 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2) + (3λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 = 2 + (3λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. So we need this to be >4 for Alice to win on her second move. So the condition is:

2 + (3λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 > 4  => (3λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2 > 2 => 3λ - sqrt(2-λ^2) > √2 (since positive) => 3λ > √2 + sqrt(2-λ^2). So the threshold λ0 is the solution to 3λ = √2 + sqrt(2-λ^2). Solve this equation.

Square both sides? Let s = sqrt(2-λ^2). Then 3λ = √2 + s. Also, s^2 = 2-λ^2. So we have (3λ - √2)^2 = s^2 = 2-λ^2. However, we need to determine the outcome for each λ.

We need to analyze the game in a more systematic way. This is a classic "alternating" game with two cumulative constraints. We can think of the game as a kind of "boundary" game where players try to force the other's bound to be exceeded. This is reminiscent of a game where two players alternately choose increments, and the game ends when one of two linear functions (in terms of cumulative sums) is exceeded. It can be analyzed using "strategy stealing" or "strategy" based on potential functions.

Idea: Let's define the "distance" from the constraints. At any point, the game state can be characterized by (S, Q) and the index n. But the constraints are only checked at certain indices. However, we can consider the game after each player's move and see if the opponent can force a loss.

Alternative approach: Consider the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. This implies that if Q_n is small, S_n is also small. The two constraints are: S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1) and Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. We can think of the game as a kind of "pursuit" where each player tries to push the other's cumulative sum over the bound. Since the constraints are linear and quadratic, we can try to find a "critical" value λ such that Alice can guarantee a win if and only if λ > λ_c, and Bazza can guarantee a win if and only if λ < λ_c. The example λ=0.8 gave a draw, which suggests that maybe λ_c = 1? But we saw λ=0.85 works for Alice, λ=0.8 draw. So perhaps the threshold is λ = 1? Let's test λ=0.9 with x1=0.9 we saw win. λ=0.85 win. λ=0.8 draw. λ=0.7 win? Actually we saw λ=0.7, Bazza wins. So there is a range where Bazza wins, a range where draw, and a range where Alice wins. So the problem likely asks to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. It might be that for λ ≤ something, Bazza wins; for λ ≥ something, Alice wins; and for an intermediate range, it's a draw. But we need to find the exact threshold.

From our tests:
λ=0.5: Bazza wins.
λ=0.7: Bazza wins.
λ=0.8: draw.
λ=0.85: Alice wins.
λ=0.9: Alice wins.
λ=1: Alice wins.
λ=1.2: Alice wins.
So the critical region seems to be around λ=0.8? But we need to be more precise. Perhaps the threshold for Bazza win is λ ≤ 2/3? And for Alice win is λ ≥ something? But 0.7 < 0.8 and Bazza wins; 0.8 draw; 0.85 Alice wins. So maybe the threshold is λ = 1? But 0.9 < 1 and Alice wins, so that's not. Maybe λ = 3√2/5 ≈ 0.8485 is the threshold where Alice can win by taking x1=λ; for λ < that, she cannot win by that strategy, but perhaps Bazza can still win by a different strategy? For λ=0.8, we saw that with the max strategy, she survives. But can Bazza force a win with a different strategy? Let's try to see if for λ=0.8, Bazza can choose x2 smaller to allow him to make a larger x4 later? For λ=0.8, if Alice takes x1=0.8, then Bazza's choice of x2 determines the future. If he takes x2 less than the max, then S2 is smaller, and Alice's x3 = 2.4 - S2 is larger, so Q3 becomes larger. That might help Bazza? But we need to see if he can eventually make S_even exceed 2λ=1.6. Let's try a different initial move by Alice: maybe she doesn't take x1=0.8; she could take x1=0 to try to force a win? But we already saw she cannot win with x1=0; the game is a draw. But maybe she can take a different x1 to allow Bazza to win? That would be bad for her. So she will choose the best strategy. So for λ=0.8, her best strategy seems to be x1=0.8, and then the game is a draw. So she doesn't have a winning strategy. But could Bazza have a winning strategy? He can choose his moves. For λ=0.8, with x1=0.8, we simulated Bazza's optimal max strategy and it gave a draw. But maybe he can deviate to cause a win? Let's examine the recurrence more closely.

For λ=0.8, we have a0=0.64, and Bazza chooses x2. He wants to eventually make S_even > λ(odd+2). That would require some x_{2k} > 1.6. The bound on x_{2k} is sqrt(2k - a_k). So he needs a_k to be small enough that sqrt(2k - a_k) > 1.6 => 2k - a_k > 2.56 => a_k < 2k - 2.56. For large k, a_k is roughly? From recurrence with max play, a_k seems to be about 2k + something. Let's analyze the recurrence for a_k under optimal play by Bazza: he chooses x_{2k+2} to minimize a_{k+1} = a_k + x^2 + (2λ - x)^2, subject to x^2 ≤ 2k+2 - a_k. Let's analyze the recurrence more generally.

The game is determined by the following: Alice chooses x1, then for each k ≥ 0, Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - a_k)], where a_k = Q_{2k+1} (the sum of squares after Alice's move at odd 2k+1). Then a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2. The game continues as long as a_k ≤ 2k+2 for each k. If at some k, a_k > 2k+2, then Bazza loses on his next turn. So Alice wants to make a_k > 2k+2 at some k. Bazza wants to avoid that.

We can view the recurrence for a_k. Note that the function h(x) = x^2 + (2λ - x)^2 is minimized at x = λ, with value 2λ^2. Its maximum on [0, B] is at the endpoints, with value min(B^2, 2λ^2?) Actually h(0)=4λ^2, h(B)=B^2+(2λ-B)^2. Since B ≥ 0, h(B) = B^2+(2λ-B)^2 = 2B^2 -4λB+4λ^2. This is a convex parabola in B. For B ≤ λ, h(B) is decreasing? Actually h'(B)=4B-4λ, so for B < λ, h'(B)<0, so h decreasing, so the minimum on [0,B] is at B (if B ≤ λ) or at λ (if B ≥ λ). So the minimal increase is:
- If B ≥ λ, then the minimal increase is 2λ^2.
- If B < λ, then the minimal increase is h(B) = B^2+(2λ-B)^2.
But note that B = sqrt(2k+2 - a_k). Since a_k is increasing, B may become less than λ eventually.

We need to find the outcome for each λ. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" game that can be solved using the idea of "critical" λ where the recurrence gives a_k = 2k+2 exactly? Or maybe the threshold is λ = 1. Let's test λ=0.71 with a different strategy by Bazza: he might choose x2 = sqrt(2-λ^2) = 1.222 (max). Then we saw Alice wins. So Bazza will not choose that. He will choose x2 = λ = 0.71, which gave a draw? But we simulated with x=λ and it seemed to lead to loss after a few steps? Let's recompute with λ=0.71 more carefully, using the recurrence with x=λ.

We have λ=0.71.

Let's analyze the recurrence more generally to find the critical λ where the outcome switches.

Given the complexity, we need a systematic method. Let's define the game after each of Alice's moves. Let's denote the state after her odd move at n=2k+1. She sets S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Let a_k = Q_{2k+1}. Then Bazza's move at even n=2k+2: he chooses x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - a_k)], and then a_{k+1} = a_k + x_{2k+2}^2 + (2λ - x_{2k+2})^2. The game continues as long as a_k ≤ 2k+2 for all k. He wants to avoid a_k > 2k+2. Alice wants to make a_k > 2k+2 at some k.

We can analyze this as a "pursuit" game where each player chooses the increment to influence future. This is reminiscent of a "strategy" based on "potential function". This is legal if 3λ - √2 ≥ 0, i.e., λ ≥ √2/3 ≈ 0.4714. For λ < 0.4714, Alice cannot even make her second move because S2 > 3λ? Then Bazza's move at n=4: he can choose x4 up to sqrt(4 - Q3). He will choose x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) to maximize S4. Then S4 = S3 + x4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - Q3). Then we check if S4 > 5λ. If so, Alice loses at n=5. So the condition for Bazza to win on his second move is:

3λ + sqrt(4 - Q3) > 5λ  => sqrt(4 - Q3) > 2λ => 4 - Q3 > 4λ^2 => Q3 < 4 - 4λ^2.

But Q3 = 2 + (3λ - √2)^2 = 2 + 9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 2 = 9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 4. So the inequality becomes:

9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 4 < 4 - 4λ^2 => 9λ^2 - 6√2 λ + 4 < 4 - 4λ^2 => 9λ^2 - 6√2 λ < -4λ^2 => 13λ^2 - 6√2 λ < 0 => λ(13λ - 6√2) < 0 => λ < 6√2/13 ≈ 0.662. So if λ < 6√2/13 ≈ 0.662, then Bazza can win by taking x2 = √2 and then x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) (the maximum). For λ = 0.662, equality, then S4 = 5λ? At λ=0.662, S4 = 3*0.662 + sqrt(4 - (9*0.662^2 - 6√2*0.662 + 4)) = 1.986 + sqrt(4 - (9*0.438 - 5.618 + 4)) = 1.986 + sqrt(4 - (3.942 - 5.618 + 4)) = 1.986 + sqrt(4 - 2.324) = 1.986 + sqrt(1.676) = 1.986 + 1.294 = 3.28, and 5λ=3.31, so S4<5λ, no win. Then Alice x5=3.3-3.276=0.024, Q5=2.320+0.0006=2.3206, then x6= sqrt(6-2.3206)= sqrt(3.6794)=1.918, S6=3.276+1.918=5.194, 7λ=4.62, so S6>4.62? Actually 5.194>4.62, so Alice loses at n=7?
